Therefore, the answer is π/12. **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{12}} </think> To convert the given triple integral from Cartesian coordinates to cylindrical coordinates, we start by analyzing the region of integration and the integrand. The integral is: \[ \int_0^1 \int_0^x \int_0^{ \sqrt{x^2+y^2}} \frac{(x^2+y^2)^{3/2}}{x^2+y^2+z^2}\,dz\,dy\,dx \] The region of integration is bounded by the cone \( z = \sqrt{x^2 + y^2} \) and the xy-plane. In cylindrical coordinates, this region is described by \( 0 \leq r \leq \frac{1}{\cos\theta} \), \( 0 \leq z \leq r \), and \( 0 \leq \theta \leq \frac{\pi}{4} \). The integrand in cylindrical coordinates becomes: \[ \frac{(r^2)^{3/2}}{r^2 + z^2} = \frac{r^3}{r^2 + z^2} \] Including the Jacobian determinant \( r \), the integral transforms to: \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz \, dr \, d\theta \] First, integrate with respect to \( z \): \[ \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz = r^4 \left[ \frac{1}{r} \arctan\left(\frac{z}{r}\right) \right]_{0}^{r} = r^4 \left( \frac{\pi}{4r} \right) = \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \] Next, integrate with respect to \( r \): \[ \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \, dr = \frac{\pi}{4} \left[ \frac{r^4}{4} \right]_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} = \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \] Finally, integrate with respect to \( \theta \): \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \frac{\pi}{16} \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta \] Using the substitution \( u = \tan\theta \), the integral of \( \sec^4\theta \) is evaluated as: \[ \int \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \tan\theta + \frac{1}{3} \tan^3\theta \Big|_{0}^{\pi/4} = 1 + \frac{1}{3} = \frac{4}{3} \] Thus, the integral simplifies to: \[ \frac{\pi}{16} \cdot \frac{4}{3} = \frac{\pi}{12} \] The final answer is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{12}} \]

Let’s use the identity sec^2θ = 1 + tan^2θ. So: = ∫ (1 + tan^2θ) sec^2θ dθ Let u = tanθ, then du = sec^2θ dθ. Then the integral becomes: ∫ (1 + u^2) du = u + (1/3)u^3 + C = tanθ + (1/3) tan^3θ + C Therefore, the integral of sec^4θ dθ from 0 to π/4 is: [ tanθ + (1/3) tan^3θ ] from 0 to π/4 At π/4: tan(π/4) = 1, so 1 + (1/3)(1)^3 = 1 + 1/3 = 4/3 At 0: tan(0) = 0, so 0 + 0 = 0 Therefore, the integral is 4/3 - 0 = 4/3 Therefore, I = (π/16) * (4/3) = π/12 So the final result is π/12. Wait, let me verify once again to make sure I didn't make any mistakes. Starting from the original integral: Converted to cylindrical coordinates, ended up with: I = ∫_{0}^{π/4} ∫_{0}^{1/cosθ} ∫_{0}^{r} [r^4 / (r^2 + z^2)] dz dr dθ Integrated over z first: Integral over z gives (π/4) r^3 Then integrated over r: (1/4) r^4 evaluated from 0 to 1/cosθ gives (1/4)(1/cosθ)^4 So I becomes (π/16) ∫_{0}^{π/4} sec^4θ dθ = (π/16)*(4/3) = π/12 That seems correct. Let me check if converting to spherical coordinates would have been easier. Let me try that approach as a sanity check. In spherical coordinates, the integral is: ∫_{0}^{π/4} ∫_{π/4}^{π/2} ∫_{0}^{1/(sinφ cosθ)} ρ^3 sin^4φ dρ dφ dθ First, integrate over ρ: ∫_{0}^{1/(sinφ cosθ)} ρ^3 dρ = [ (1/4) ρ^4 ] from 0 to 1/(sinφ cosθ) = (1/4)(1/(sinφ cosθ))^4 So the integral becomes: (1/4) ∫_{0}^{π/4} ∫_{π/4}^{π/2} (1/(sinφ cosθ))^4 sin^4φ dφ dθ Simplify the integrand: (1/(sinφ cosθ))^4 * sin^4φ = (1/sin^4φ cos^4θ) * sin^4φ = 1/(cos^4θ) * 1 Therefore, the integral becomes: (1/4) ∫_{0}^{π/4} [ ∫_{π/4}^{π/2} dφ ] / cos^4θ dθ But wait, integrating over φ from π/4 to π/2: ∫_{π/4}^{π/2} dφ = π/2 - π/4 = π/4 Therefore, the integral simplifies to: (1/4) * (π/4) ∫_{0}^{π/4} 1/cos^4θ dθ = (π/16) ∫_{0}^{π/4} sec^4θ dθ Which is exactly the same as in cylindrical coordinates! Therefore, both coordinate systems lead to the same result, but cylindrical coordinates perhaps made it a bit more straightforward because the limits for z were simpler. So in conclusion, the integral simplifies to π/12 when converted to cylindrical coordinates (or spherical, but cylindrical was perhaps easier here). Therefore, the answer is π/12. **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{12}} </think> To convert the given triple integral from Cartesian coordinates to cylindrical coordinates, we start by analyzing the region of integration and the integrand. The integral is: \[ \int_0^1 \int_0^x \int_0^{ \sqrt{x^2+y^2}} \frac{(x^2+y^2)^{3/2}}{x^2+y^2+z^2}\,dz\,dy\,dx \] The region of integration is bounded by the cone \( z = \sqrt{x^2 + y^2} \) and the xy-plane. In cylindrical coordinates, this region is described by \( 0 \leq r \leq \frac{1}{\cos\theta} \), \( 0 \leq z \leq r \), and \( 0 \leq \theta \leq \frac{\pi}{4} \). The integrand in cylindrical coordinates becomes: \[ \frac{(r^2)^{3/2}}{r^2 + z^2} = \frac{r^3}{r^2 + z^2} \] Including the Jacobian determinant \( r \), the integral transforms to: \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz \, dr \, d\theta \] First, integrate with respect to \( z \): \[ \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz = r^4 \left[ \frac{1}{r} \arctan\left(\frac{z}{r}\right) \right]_{0}^{r} = r^4 \left( \frac{\pi}{4r} \right) = \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \] Next, integrate with respect to \( r \): \[ \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \, dr = \frac{\pi}{4} \left[ \frac{r^4}{4} \right]_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} = \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \] Finally, integrate with respect to \( \theta \): \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \frac{\pi}{16} \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta \] Using the substitution \( u = \tan\theta \), the integral of \( \sec^4\theta \) is evaluated as: \[ \int \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \tan\theta + \frac{1}{3} \tan^3\theta \Big|_{0}^{\pi/4} = 1 + \frac{1}{3} = \frac{4}{3} \] Thus, the integral simplifies to: \[ \frac{\pi}{16} \cdot \frac{4}{3} = \frac{\pi}{12} \] The final answer is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{12}} \]

Therefore, the answer is π/12. **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{12}} </think> To convert the given triple integral from Cartesian coordinates to cylindrical coordinates, we start by analyzing the region of integration and the integrand. The integral is: \[ \int_0^1 \int_0^x \int_0^{ \sqrt{x^2+y^2}} \frac{(x^2+y^2)^{3/2}}{x^2+y^2+z^2}\,dz\,dy\,dx \] The region of integration is bounded by the cone \( z = \sqrt{x^2 + y^2} \) and the xy-plane. In cylindrical coordinates, this region is described by \( 0 \leq r \leq \frac{1}{\cos\theta} \), \( 0 \leq z \leq r \), and \( 0 \leq \theta \leq \frac{\pi}{4} \). The integrand in cylindrical coordinates becomes: \[ \frac{(r^2)^{3/2}}{r^2 + z^2} = \frac{r^3}{r^2 + z^2} \] Including the Jacobian determinant \( r \), the integral transforms to: \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz \, dr \, d\theta \] First, integrate with respect to \( z \): \[ \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz = r^4 \left[ \frac{1}{r} \arctan\left(\frac{z}{r}\right) \right]_{0}^{r} = r^4 \left( \frac{\pi}{4r} \right) = \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \] Next, integrate with respect to \( r \): \[ \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \, dr = \frac{\pi}{4} \left[ \frac{r^4}{4} \right]_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} = \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \] Finally, integrate with respect to \( \theta \): \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \frac{\pi}{16} \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta \] Using the substitution \( u = \tan\theta \), the integral of \( \sec^4\theta \) is evaluated as: \[ \int \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \tan\theta + \frac{1}{3} \tan^3\theta \Big|_{0}^{\pi/4} = 1 + \frac{1}{3} = \frac{4}{3} \] Thus, the integral simplifies to: \[ \frac{\pi}{16} \cdot \frac{4}{3} = \frac{\pi}{12} \] The final answer is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{12}} \]

The integrand in cylindrical coordinates becomes: \[ \frac{(r^2)^{3/2}}{r^2 + z^2} = \frac{r^3}{r^2 + z^2} \] Including the Jacobian determinant \( r \), the integral transforms to: \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz \, dr \, d\theta \] First, integrate with respect to \( z \): \[ \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz = r^4 \left[ \frac{1}{r} \arctan\left(\frac{z}{r}\right) \right]_{0}^{r} = r^4 \left( \frac{\pi}{4r} \right) = \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \] Next, integrate with respect to \( r \): \[ \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \, dr = \frac{\pi}{4} \left[ \frac{r^4}{4} \right]_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} = \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \] Finally, integrate with respect to \( \theta \): \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \frac{\pi}{16} \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta \] Using the substitution \( u = \tan\theta \), the integral of \( \sec^4\theta \) is evaluated as: \[ \int \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \tan\theta + \frac{1}{3} \tan^3\theta \Big|_{0}^{\pi/4} = 1 + \frac{1}{3} = \frac{4}{3} \] Thus, the integral simplifies to: \[ \frac{\pi}{16} \cdot \frac{4}{3} = \frac{\pi}{12} \] The final answer is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{12}} \]

The integral is: \[ \int_0^1 \int_0^x \int_0^{ \sqrt{x^2+y^2}} \frac{(x^2+y^2)^{3/2}}{x^2+y^2+z^2}\,dz\,dy\,dx \] The region of integration is bounded by the cone \( z = \sqrt{x^2 + y^2} \) and the xy-plane. In cylindrical coordinates, this region is described by \( 0 \leq r \leq \frac{1}{\cos\theta} \), \( 0 \leq z \leq r \), and \( 0 \leq \theta \leq \frac{\pi}{4} \). The integrand in cylindrical coordinates becomes: \[ \frac{(r^2)^{3/2}}{r^2 + z^2} = \frac{r^3}{r^2 + z^2} \] Including the Jacobian determinant \( r \), the integral transforms to: \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz \, dr \, d\theta \] First, integrate with respect to \( z \): \[ \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz = r^4 \left[ \frac{1}{r} \arctan\left(\frac{z}{r}\right) \right]_{0}^{r} = r^4 \left( \frac{\pi}{4r} \right) = \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \] Next, integrate with respect to \( r \): \[ \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \, dr = \frac{\pi}{4} \left[ \frac{r^4}{4} \right]_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} = \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \] Finally, integrate with respect to \( \theta \): \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \frac{\pi}{16} \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta \] Using the substitution \( u = \tan\theta \), the integral of \( \sec^4\theta \) is evaluated as: \[ \int \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \tan\theta + \frac{1}{3} \tan^3\theta \Big|_{0}^{\pi/4} = 1 + \frac{1}{3} = \frac{4}{3} \] Thus, the integral simplifies to: \[ \frac{\pi}{16} \cdot \frac{4}{3} = \frac{\pi}{12} \] The final answer is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{12}} \]

The integral is: \[ \int_0^1 \int_0^x \int_0^{ \sqrt{x^2+y^2}} \frac{(x^2+y^2)^{3/2}}{x^2+y^2+z^2}\,dz\,dy\,dx \] The region of integration is bounded by the cone \( z = \sqrt{x^2 + y^2} \) and the xy-plane. In cylindrical coordinates, this region is described by \( 0 \leq r \leq \frac{1}{\cos\theta} \), \( 0 \leq z \leq r \), and \( 0 \leq \theta \leq \frac{\pi}{4} \). The integrand in cylindrical coordinates becomes: \[ \frac{(r^2)^{3/2}}{r^2 + z^2} = \frac{r^3}{r^2 + z^2} \] Including the Jacobian determinant \( r \), the integral transforms to: \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz \, dr \, d\theta \] First, integrate with respect to \( z \): \[ \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz = r^4 \left[ \frac{1}{r} \arctan\left(\frac{z}{r}\right) \right]_{0}^{r} = r^4 \left( \frac{\pi}{4r} \right) = \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \] Next, integrate with respect to \( r \): \[ \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \, dr = \frac{\pi}{4} \left[ \frac{r^4}{4} \right]_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} = \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \] Finally, integrate with respect to \( \theta \): \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \frac{\pi}{16} \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta \] Using the substitution \( u = \tan\theta \), the integral of \( \sec^4\theta \) is evaluated as: \[ \int \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \tan\theta + \frac{1}{3} \tan^3\theta \Big|_{0}^{\pi/4} = 1 + \frac{1}{3} = \frac{4}{3} \] Thus, the integral simplifies to: \[ \frac{\pi}{16} \cdot \frac{4}{3} = \frac{\pi}{12} \] The final answer is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{12}} \]

Therefore, the answer is π/12. **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{12}} </think> To convert the given triple integral from Cartesian coordinates to cylindrical coordinates, we start by analyzing the region of integration and the integrand. The integral is: \[ \int_0^1 \int_0^x \int_0^{ \sqrt{x^2+y^2}} \frac{(x^2+y^2)^{3/2}}{x^2+y^2+z^2}\,dz\,dy\,dx \] The region of integration is bounded by the cone \( z = \sqrt{x^2 + y^2} \) and the xy-plane. In cylindrical coordinates, this region is described by \( 0 \leq r \leq \frac{1}{\cos\theta} \), \( 0 \leq z \leq r \), and \( 0 \leq \theta \leq \frac{\pi}{4} \). The integrand in cylindrical coordinates becomes: \[ \frac{(r^2)^{3/2}}{r^2 + z^2} = \frac{r^3}{r^2 + z^2} \] Including the Jacobian determinant \( r \), the integral transforms to: \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz \, dr \, d\theta \] First, integrate with respect to \( z \): \[ \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz = r^4 \left[ \frac{1}{r} \arctan\left(\frac{z}{r}\right) \right]_{0}^{r} = r^4 \left( \frac{\pi}{4r} \right) = \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \] Next, integrate with respect to \( r \): \[ \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \, dr = \frac{\pi}{4} \left[ \frac{r^4}{4} \right]_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} = \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \] Finally, integrate with respect to \( \theta \): \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \frac{\pi}{16} \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta \] Using the substitution \( u = \tan\theta \), the integral of \( \sec^4\theta \) is evaluated as: \[ \int \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \tan\theta + \frac{1}{3} \tan^3\theta \Big|_{0}^{\pi/4} = 1 + \frac{1}{3} = \frac{4}{3} \] Thus, the integral simplifies to: \[ \frac{\pi}{16} \cdot \frac{4}{3} = \frac{\pi}{12} \] The final answer is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{12}} \]

So indeed, the spherical coordinate approach also leads to the same integral, which evaluates to π/12. Therefore, both coordinate systems lead to the same result, but cylindrical coordinates perhaps made it a bit more straightforward because the limits for z were simpler. So in conclusion, the integral simplifies to π/12 when converted to cylindrical coordinates (or spherical, but cylindrical was perhaps easier here). Therefore, the answer is π/12. **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{12}} </think> To convert the given triple integral from Cartesian coordinates to cylindrical coordinates, we start by analyzing the region of integration and the integrand. The integral is: \[ \int_0^1 \int_0^x \int_0^{ \sqrt{x^2+y^2}} \frac{(x^2+y^2)^{3/2}}{x^2+y^2+z^2}\,dz\,dy\,dx \] The region of integration is bounded by the cone \( z = \sqrt{x^2 + y^2} \) and the xy-plane. In cylindrical coordinates, this region is described by \( 0 \leq r \leq \frac{1}{\cos\theta} \), \( 0 \leq z \leq r \), and \( 0 \leq \theta \leq \frac{\pi}{4} \). The integrand in cylindrical coordinates becomes: \[ \frac{(r^2)^{3/2}}{r^2 + z^2} = \frac{r^3}{r^2 + z^2} \] Including the Jacobian determinant \( r \), the integral transforms to: \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz \, dr \, d\theta \] First, integrate with respect to \( z \): \[ \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz = r^4 \left[ \frac{1}{r} \arctan\left(\frac{z}{r}\right) \right]_{0}^{r} = r^4 \left( \frac{\pi}{4r} \right) = \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \] Next, integrate with respect to \( r \): \[ \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \, dr = \frac{\pi}{4} \left[ \frac{r^4}{4} \right]_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} = \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \] Finally, integrate with respect to \( \theta \): \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \frac{\pi}{16} \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta \] Using the substitution \( u = \tan\theta \), the integral of \( \sec^4\theta \) is evaluated as: \[ \int \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \tan\theta + \frac{1}{3} \tan^3\theta \Big|_{0}^{\pi/4} = 1 + \frac{1}{3} = \frac{4}{3} \] Thus, the integral simplifies to: \[ \frac{\pi}{16} \cdot \frac{4}{3} = \frac{\pi}{12} \] The final answer is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{12}} \]

The integral is: \[ \int_0^1 \int_0^x \int_0^{ \sqrt{x^2+y^2}} \frac{(x^2+y^2)^{3/2}}{x^2+y^2+z^2}\,dz\,dy\,dx \] The region of integration is bounded by the cone \( z = \sqrt{x^2 + y^2} \) and the xy-plane. In cylindrical coordinates, this region is described by \( 0 \leq r \leq \frac{1}{\cos\theta} \), \( 0 \leq z \leq r \), and \( 0 \leq \theta \leq \frac{\pi}{4} \). The integrand in cylindrical coordinates becomes: \[ \frac{(r^2)^{3/2}}{r^2 + z^2} = \frac{r^3}{r^2 + z^2} \] Including the Jacobian determinant \( r \), the integral transforms to: \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz \, dr \, d\theta \] First, integrate with respect to \( z \): \[ \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz = r^4 \left[ \frac{1}{r} \arctan\left(\frac{z}{r}\right) \right]_{0}^{r} = r^4 \left( \frac{\pi}{4r} \right) = \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \] Next, integrate with respect to \( r \): \[ \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \, dr = \frac{\pi}{4} \left[ \frac{r^4}{4} \right]_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} = \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \] Finally, integrate with respect to \( \theta \): \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \frac{\pi}{16} \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta \] Using the substitution \( u = \tan\theta \), the integral of \( \sec^4\theta \) is evaluated as: \[ \int \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \tan\theta + \frac{1}{3} \tan^3\theta \Big|_{0}^{\pi/4} = 1 + \frac{1}{3} = \frac{4}{3} \] Thus, the integral simplifies to: \[ \frac{\pi}{16} \cdot \frac{4}{3} = \frac{\pi}{12} \] The final answer is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{12}} \]

Therefore, the answer is π/12. **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{12}} </think> To convert the given triple integral from Cartesian coordinates to cylindrical coordinates, we start by analyzing the region of integration and the integrand. The integral is: \[ \int_0^1 \int_0^x \int_0^{ \sqrt{x^2+y^2}} \frac{(x^2+y^2)^{3/2}}{x^2+y^2+z^2}\,dz\,dy\,dx \] The region of integration is bounded by the cone \( z = \sqrt{x^2 + y^2} \) and the xy-plane. In cylindrical coordinates, this region is described by \( 0 \leq r \leq \frac{1}{\cos\theta} \), \( 0 \leq z \leq r \), and \( 0 \leq \theta \leq \frac{\pi}{4} \). The integrand in cylindrical coordinates becomes: \[ \frac{(r^2)^{3/2}}{r^2 + z^2} = \frac{r^3}{r^2 + z^2} \] Including the Jacobian determinant \( r \), the integral transforms to: \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz \, dr \, d\theta \] First, integrate with respect to \( z \): \[ \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz = r^4 \left[ \frac{1}{r} \arctan\left(\frac{z}{r}\right) \right]_{0}^{r} = r^4 \left( \frac{\pi}{4r} \right) = \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \] Next, integrate with respect to \( r \): \[ \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \, dr = \frac{\pi}{4} \left[ \frac{r^4}{4} \right]_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} = \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \] Finally, integrate with respect to \( \theta \): \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \frac{\pi}{16} \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta \] Using the substitution \( u = \tan\theta \), the integral of \( \sec^4\theta \) is evaluated as: \[ \int \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \tan\theta + \frac{1}{3} \tan^3\theta \Big|_{0}^{\pi/4} = 1 + \frac{1}{3} = \frac{4}{3} \] Thus, the integral simplifies to: \[ \frac{\pi}{16} \cdot \frac{4}{3} = \frac{\pi}{12} \] The final answer is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{12}} \]

Therefore, both coordinate systems lead to the same result, but cylindrical coordinates perhaps made it a bit more straightforward because the limits for z were simpler. So in conclusion, the integral simplifies to π/12 when converted to cylindrical coordinates (or spherical, but cylindrical was perhaps easier here). The integral is: \[ \int_0^1 \int_0^x \int_0^{ \sqrt{x^2+y^2}} \frac{(x^2+y^2)^{3/2}}{x^2+y^2+z^2}\,dz\,dy\,dx \] The region of integration is bounded by the cone \( z = \sqrt{x^2 + y^2} \) and the xy-plane. In cylindrical coordinates, this region is described by \( 0 \leq r \leq \frac{1}{\cos\theta} \), \( 0 \leq z \leq r \), and \( 0 \leq \theta \leq \frac{\pi}{4} \). The integrand in cylindrical coordinates becomes: \[ \frac{(r^2)^{3/2}}{r^2 + z^2} = \frac{r^3}{r^2 + z^2} \] Including the Jacobian determinant \( r \), the integral transforms to: \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz \, dr \, d\theta \] First, integrate with respect to \( z \): \[ \int_{0}^{r} \frac{r^4}{r^2 + z^2} \, dz = r^4 \left[ \frac{1}{r} \arctan\left(\frac{z}{r}\right) \right]_{0}^{r} = r^4 \left( \frac{\pi}{4r} \right) = \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \] Next, integrate with respect to \( r \): \[ \int_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} \frac{\pi}{4} r^3 \, dr = \frac{\pi}{4} \left[ \frac{r^4}{4} \right]_{0}^{1/\cos\theta} = \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \] Finally, integrate with respect to \( \theta \): \[ \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \frac{\pi}{16} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \frac{\pi}{16} \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \sec^4\theta \, d\theta \] Using the substitution \( u = \tan\theta \), the integral of \( \sec^4\theta \) is evaluated as: \[ \int \sec^4\theta \, d\theta = \tan\theta + \frac{1}{3} \tan^3\theta \Big|_{0}^{\pi/4} = 1 + \frac{1}{3} = \frac{4}{3} \] Thus, the integral simplifies to: \[ \frac{\pi}{16} \cdot \frac{4}{3} = \frac{\pi}{12} \] The final answer is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{12}} \]

Problem: Let $(X_{n})_{n\geq1}$ be a sequence of i.i.d. random variables such that $\mathbb{P}(X_{n} = -1) = \frac{1}{3}$ and $\mathbb{P}(X_{n} = 1) = \frac{2}{3}$. Let $S_{0} = 0$ and for $n\geq 1$ let $S_{n} = \sum_{k=1}^{n} X_{k}$. Determine the value of $\mathbb{P}(\liminf S_{n} = -\infty)$. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to figure out the probability that the liminf of the random walk \( S_n \) is \(-\infty\). The problem states that each \( X_n \) is an i.i.d. random variable with \( \mathbb{P}(X_n = -1) = \frac{1}{3} \) and \( \mathbb{P}(X_n = 1) = \frac{2}{3} \). The process starts at \( S_0 = 0 \), and for each \( n \geq 1 \), \( S_n = \sum_{k=1}^n X_k \). The question is asking for \( \mathbb{P}(\liminf S_n = -\infty) \). First, let me recall what liminf means. The liminf of a sequence \( S_n \) as \( n \to \infty \) is the smallest limit point of the sequence. In other words, it's the infimum of all the limit points that the sequence approaches infinitely often. So if \( \liminf S_n = -\infty \), that means the sequence \( S_n \) goes arbitrarily low infinitely often. In the context of a random walk, this would imply that the walk recurrently visits every negative level, no matter how far down. But wait, in probability theory, when we talk about liminf and limsup of random walks, we often classify them as either transient or recurrent. A recurrent random walk returns to its starting point infinitely often with probability 1, whereas a transient random walk eventually leaves forever with probability 1. However, here we're dealing with liminf behavior, which is a bit different. The question is about whether the walk goes to \(-\infty\) infinitely often, not necessarily returning to a specific point. Given that the steps are i.i.d., each step has a drift. Let me compute the expected value of each step \( X_n \). The expectation is \( \mathbb{E}[X_n] = (-1) \times \frac{1}{3} + 1 \times \frac{2}{3} = \frac{-1 + 2}{3} = \frac{1}{3} \). So the drift is positive, which suggests that the walk has a tendency to drift towards \(+\infty\). But the question is about the liminf being \(-\infty\). Even if the walk drifts to \(+\infty\), it might still have liminf \(-\infty\) if it fluctuates enough downwards along the way. However, with a positive drift, maybe such fluctuations are not strong enough? In one-dimensional random walks, there's a concept of whether the walk is recurrent or transient. For symmetric simple random walks (like with equal probability to go left or right), the walk is recurrent in 1D and 2D, but transient in higher dimensions. However, in our case, the walk is not symmetric; it has a drift towards the right (positive direction). So perhaps it's transient to the right, meaning that it tends to \(+\infty\) as \( n \to \infty \), but the liminf could still be \(-\infty\) or finite. Wait, but the liminf is the infimum of the limit points. So even if the walk goes to \(+\infty\), but it fluctuates a lot, the liminf could be finite. For example, if the walk goes to \(+\infty\) but every once in a while comes back down to some finite level, the liminf would be that finite level. But if it can go arbitrarily low infinitely often, then the liminf is \(-\infty\). So the key is to determine whether, with probability 1, the random walk \( S_n \) will reach any negative level infinitely often. If yes, then the liminf is \(-\infty\). If no, then the liminf is some finite value. This positive drift indicates a tendency towards \(+\infty\). 2. **Strong Law of Large Numbers (SLLN)**: - By the SLLN, \(\frac{S_n}{n} \to \frac{1}{3}\) almost surely. This implies \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely. 3. **Liminf Behavior**: - If \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, then for any large \(M\), there exists \(N\) such that for all \(n \geq N\), \(S_n \geq M\). Therefore, both \(\liminf S_n\) and \(\limsup S_n\) are \(+\infty\). 4. **Probability Analysis**: - The liminf of \(S_n\) being \(-\infty\) would require the walk to visit arbitrarily low levels infinitely often. However, due to the positive drift, the walk tends to \(+\infty\) almost surely, making such events impossible. - Using the Borel-Cantelli lemma, the probability of the walk reaching any negative level \(-k\) is \(\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)^k\), which is summable. Hence, the probability of infinitely many such events is 0. 5. **Conclusion**: - Since \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, the liminf of \(S_n\) is also \(+\infty\). Therefore, \(\mathbb{P}(\liminf S_{n} = -\infty) = 0\). \[ \boxed{0} \]

Hence, the probability of infinitely many such events is 0. 5. **Conclusion**: - Since \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, the liminf of \(S_n\) is also \(+\infty\). Therefore, \(\mathbb{P}(\liminf S_{n} = -\infty) = 0\). \[ \boxed{0} \]

This positive drift indicates a tendency towards \(+\infty\). 2. **Strong Law of Large Numbers (SLLN)**: - By the SLLN, \(\frac{S_n}{n} \to \frac{1}{3}\) almost surely. This implies \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely. 3. **Liminf Behavior**: - If \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, then for any large \(M\), there exists \(N\) such that for all \(n \geq N\), \(S_n \geq M\). Therefore, both \(\liminf S_n\) and \(\limsup S_n\) are \(+\infty\). 4. **Probability Analysis**: - The liminf of \(S_n\) being \(-\infty\) would require the walk to visit arbitrarily low levels infinitely often. However, due to the positive drift, the walk tends to \(+\infty\) almost surely, making such events impossible. - Using the Borel-Cantelli lemma, the probability of the walk reaching any negative level \(-k\) is \(\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)^k\), which is summable. Hence, the probability of infinitely many such events is 0. 5. **Conclusion**: - Since \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, the liminf of \(S_n\) is also \(+\infty\). Therefore, \(\mathbb{P}(\liminf S_{n} = -\infty) = 0\). \[ \boxed{0} \]

This positive drift indicates a tendency towards \(+\infty\). 2. **Strong Law of Large Numbers (SLLN)**: - By the SLLN, \(\frac{S_n}{n} \to \frac{1}{3}\) almost surely. This implies \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely. 3. **Liminf Behavior**: - If \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, then for any large \(M\), there exists \(N\) such that for all \(n \geq N\), \(S_n \geq M\). Therefore, both \(\liminf S_n\) and \(\limsup S_n\) are \(+\infty\). 4. **Probability Analysis**: - The liminf of \(S_n\) being \(-\infty\) would require the walk to visit arbitrarily low levels infinitely often. However, due to the positive drift, the walk tends to \(+\infty\) almost surely, making such events impossible. - Using the Borel-Cantelli lemma, the probability of the walk reaching any negative level \(-k\) is \(\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)^k\), which is summable. Hence, the probability of infinitely many such events is 0. 5. **Conclusion**: - Since \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, the liminf of \(S_n\) is also \(+\infty\). Therefore, \(\mathbb{P}(\liminf S_{n} = -\infty) = 0\). \[ \boxed{0} \]

Hence, the probability of infinitely many such events is 0. 5. **Conclusion**: - Since \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, the liminf of \(S_n\) is also \(+\infty\). Therefore, \(\mathbb{P}(\liminf S_{n} = -\infty) = 0\). \[ \boxed{0} \]

This positive drift indicates a tendency towards \(+\infty\). 2. **Strong Law of Large Numbers (SLLN)**: - By the SLLN, \(\frac{S_n}{n} \to \frac{1}{3}\) almost surely. This implies \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely. 3. **Liminf Behavior**: - If \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, then for any large \(M\), there exists \(N\) such that for all \(n \geq N\), \(S_n \geq M\). Therefore, both \(\liminf S_n\) and \(\limsup S_n\) are \(+\infty\). 4. **Probability Analysis**: - The liminf of \(S_n\) being \(-\infty\) would require the walk to visit arbitrarily low levels infinitely often. However, due to the positive drift, the walk tends to \(+\infty\) almost surely, making such events impossible. - Using the Borel-Cantelli lemma, the probability of the walk reaching any negative level \(-k\) is \(\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)^k\), which is summable. Hence, the probability of infinitely many such events is 0. 5. **Conclusion**: - Since \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, the liminf of \(S_n\) is also \(+\infty\). Therefore, \(\mathbb{P}(\liminf S_{n} = -\infty) = 0\). \[ \boxed{0} \]

Therefore, \(\mathbb{P}(\liminf S_{n} = -\infty) = 0\). \[ \boxed{0} \]

This positive drift indicates a tendency towards \(+\infty\). 2. **Strong Law of Large Numbers (SLLN)**: - By the SLLN, \(\frac{S_n}{n} \to \frac{1}{3}\) almost surely. This implies \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely. 3. **Liminf Behavior**: - If \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, then for any large \(M\), there exists \(N\) such that for all \(n \geq N\), \(S_n \geq M\). Therefore, both \(\liminf S_n\) and \(\limsup S_n\) are \(+\infty\). 4. **Probability Analysis**: - The liminf of \(S_n\) being \(-\infty\) would require the walk to visit arbitrarily low levels infinitely often. However, due to the positive drift, the walk tends to \(+\infty\) almost surely, making such events impossible. - Using the Borel-Cantelli lemma, the probability of the walk reaching any negative level \(-k\) is \(\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)^k\), which is summable. Hence, the probability of infinitely many such events is 0. 5. **Conclusion**: - Since \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, the liminf of \(S_n\) is also \(+\infty\). Therefore, \(\mathbb{P}(\liminf S_{n} = -\infty) = 0\). \[ \boxed{0} \]

This positive drift indicates a tendency towards \(+\infty\). 2. **Strong Law of Large Numbers (SLLN)**: - By the SLLN, \(\frac{S_n}{n} \to \frac{1}{3}\) almost surely. This implies \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely. 3. **Liminf Behavior**: - If \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, then for any large \(M\), there exists \(N\) such that for all \(n \geq N\), \(S_n \geq M\). Therefore, both \(\liminf S_n\) and \(\limsup S_n\) are \(+\infty\). 4. **Probability Analysis**: - The liminf of \(S_n\) being \(-\infty\) would require the walk to visit arbitrarily low levels infinitely often. However, due to the positive drift, the walk tends to \(+\infty\) almost surely, making such events impossible. - Using the Borel-Cantelli lemma, the probability of the walk reaching any negative level \(-k\) is \(\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)^k\), which is summable. Hence, the probability of infinitely many such events is 0. 5. **Conclusion**: - Since \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, the liminf of \(S_n\) is also \(+\infty\). Therefore, \(\mathbb{P}(\liminf S_{n} = -\infty) = 0\). \[ \boxed{0} \]

We know from the previous computation that \( \mathbb{P}(A_k) \leq \mathbb{P}( \text{ever reaching } -k ) = (1/2)^k \). But as \( k \to \infty \), \( (1/2)^k \to 0 \). Hence, the probability of infinitely many such events is 0. 5. **Conclusion**: - Since \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, the liminf of \(S_n\) is also \(+\infty\). Therefore, \(\mathbb{P}(\liminf S_{n} = -\infty) = 0\). \[ \boxed{0} \]

This positive drift indicates a tendency towards \(+\infty\). 2. **Strong Law of Large Numbers (SLLN)**: - By the SLLN, \(\frac{S_n}{n} \to \frac{1}{3}\) almost surely. This implies \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely. 3. **Liminf Behavior**: - If \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, then for any large \(M\), there exists \(N\) such that for all \(n \geq N\), \(S_n \geq M\). Therefore, both \(\liminf S_n\) and \(\limsup S_n\) are \(+\infty\). 4. **Probability Analysis**: - The liminf of \(S_n\) being \(-\infty\) would require the walk to visit arbitrarily low levels infinitely often. However, due to the positive drift, the walk tends to \(+\infty\) almost surely, making such events impossible. - Using the Borel-Cantelli lemma, the probability of the walk reaching any negative level \(-k\) is \(\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)^k\), which is summable. Hence, the probability of infinitely many such events is 0. 5. **Conclusion**: - Since \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, the liminf of \(S_n\) is also \(+\infty\). Therefore, \(\mathbb{P}(\liminf S_{n} = -\infty) = 0\). \[ \boxed{0} \]

Therefore, the liminf would be the minimum value achieved by the walk after that finite time, which is positive, and since the walk tends to infinity, the liminf would be infinity. Therefore, the probability that liminf is -infty would be 0. Alternatively, maybe in higher dimensions, but in 1D, once the walk is transient, it can't oscillate between positive and negative infinitely often. Wait, let me check with a concrete example. Suppose we have a biased random walk on the integers with p=2/3 and q=1/3 starting at 0. What is the probability that the walk ever reaches -1? In general, for a biased random walk starting at 0, the probability of ever reaching -1 can be computed. Let me denote this probability as \( \rho \). In the Gambler's ruin problem, the probability of ruin starting from 0, with absorbing barriers at 0 and N, but here we have an absorbing barrier at -1 and the other at infinity. Wait, but actually, if we want the probability of ever reaching -1 starting from 0, with probability p=2/3 of moving right and q=1/3 of moving left, then the probability \( \rho \) satisfies the equation: \( \rho = q + p \rho^2 \) This is because starting from 0, with probability q=1/3, we go to -1 and are ruined, and with probability p=2/3, we go to +1, and then we need to reach -1 from +1, which is equivalent to the probability \( \rho^2 \) (since we have to go from +1 to 0 and then from 0 to -1, each with probability \( \rho \)). Therefore, solving the equation \( \rho = 1/3 + (2/3) \rho^2 \). Multiply both sides by 3: \( 3\rho = 1 + 2\rho^2 \), which becomes \( 2\rho^2 - 3\rho + 1 = 0 \). Solving the quadratic equation: \( \rho = [3 \pm \sqrt{9 - 8}]/4 = [3 \pm 1]/4 \), so roots are 1 and 1/2. Since we are looking for the probability of ruin, which is less than 1 (because the drift is positive), we take \( \rho = 1/2 \). Therefore, the probability that the walk ever reaches -1 is 1/2. Therefore, the probability that it ever reaches -k would be \( (1/2)^k \), as each subsequent level would require another independent ruin. But in this case, the walk has a positive probability of eventually going to any negative level, but the probability of going to -k is \( (1/2)^k \), which is summable over k. Therefore, by Borel-Cantelli, since the sum \( \sum_{k=1}^\infty \mathbb{P}( \text{reach } -k ) = \sum_{k=1}^\infty (1/2)^k = 1 \), which converges. Wait, but Borel-Cantelli says that if the sum of probabilities is finite, then the probability that infinitely many of the events occur is 0. But here, the events are "the walk reaches -k", but these are not independent events. Wait, but actually, each time the walk reaches -k, it implies that it has reached -1, -2, ..., -k. So the events are nested. Therefore, the event that the walk reaches -k infinitely often is equivalent to the walk going to -infty. But since the probability of reaching -k is \( (1/2)^k \), the probability that it reaches -k for infinitely many k is the limit as k approaches infinity of \( (1/2)^k \), which is 0. Therefore, by Borel-Cantelli lemma (the first one), since the sum of probabilities is finite, the probability that infinitely many of these events occur is 0. Therefore, the walk does not go to -infty. Therefore, the liminf cannot be -infty. Alternatively, even if the walk has a positive drift, can it still have liminf -infty? Wait, if the walk goes to +infty a.s., then the liminf is +infty. If the walk had oscillating behavior, like in the null recurrent case (symmetric walk), then liminf is -infty and limsup is +infty. But in our case, the walk is transient to +infty, so it cannot have liminf -infty. Therefore, the answer should be 0. But let me check another approach. Let's consider the events \( A_k = \{ S_n \leq -k \text{ infinitely often} \} \). Then, \( \liminf S_n = -\infty \) is equivalent to \( \bigcap_{k=1}^\infty A_k \). We need to compute \( \mathbb{P}( \bigcap_{k=1}^\infty A_k ) \). By the continuity of probability measures, this is the limit as \( k \to \infty \) of \( \mathbb{P}(A_k) \). But \( A_{k+1} \subseteq A_k \), so the sequence \( \mathbb{P}(A_k) \) is non-increasing. Therefore, \( \mathbb{P}(\liminf S_n = -\infty) = \lim_{k \to \infty} \mathbb{P}(A_k) \). We know from the previous computation that \( \mathbb{P}(A_k) \leq \mathbb{P}( \text{ever reaching } -k ) = (1/2)^k \). But as \( k \to \infty \), \( (1/2)^k \to 0 \). Therefore, the probability \( \mathbb{P}(A_k) \to 0 \). Therefore, \( \mathbb{P}(\liminf S_n = -\infty) = 0 \). Alternatively, another way is to note that since \( S_n \to +\infty \) a.s., then for almost every sample path, there exists some integer \( K \) such that for all \( n \geq K \), \( S_n \geq 0 \). Therefore, the liminf is at least 0. But actually, since \( S_n \to +\infty \), for any \( M > 0 \), there exists \( K \) such that for all \( n \geq K \), \( S_n \geq M \). Therefore, the liminf is infinity. Therefore, the probability that the liminf is -infty is 0. So, after all this reasoning, I think the answer is 0. **Final Answer** \boxed{0} </think> Given a sequence \((X_{n})_{n \geq 1}\) of i.i.d. random variables where \(\mathbb{P}(X_{n} = -1) = \frac{1}{3}\) and \(\mathbb{P}(X_{n} = 1) = \frac{2}{3}\), we need to determine the value of \(\mathbb{P}(\liminf S_{n} = -\infty)\) for the random walk \(S_{n} = \sum_{k=1}^{n} X_{k}\) starting at \(S_{0} = 0\). 1. **Expectation and Drift**: - The expectation of each step \(X_n\) is \(\mathbb{E}[X_n] = \frac{1}{3}\). This positive drift indicates a tendency towards \(+\infty\). 2. **Strong Law of Large Numbers (SLLN)**: - By the SLLN, \(\frac{S_n}{n} \to \frac{1}{3}\) almost surely. This implies \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely. 3. **Liminf Behavior**: - If \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, then for any large \(M\), there exists \(N\) such that for all \(n \geq N\), \(S_n \geq M\). Therefore, both \(\liminf S_n\) and \(\limsup S_n\) are \(+\infty\). 4. **Probability Analysis**: - The liminf of \(S_n\) being \(-\infty\) would require the walk to visit arbitrarily low levels infinitely often. However, due to the positive drift, the walk tends to \(+\infty\) almost surely, making such events impossible. - Using the Borel-Cantelli lemma, the probability of the walk reaching any negative level \(-k\) is \(\left(\frac{1}{2}\right)^k\), which is summable. Hence, the probability of infinitely many such events is 0. 5. **Conclusion**: - Since \(S_n \to +\infty\) almost surely, the liminf of \(S_n\) is also \(+\infty\). Therefore, \(\mathbb{P}(\liminf S_{n} = -\infty) = 0\). \[ \boxed{0} \]

The lack of boundary conditions allows for non-unique solutions, some of which are non-radial. Thus, the answer is \(\boxed{No}\).

Any harmonic function (satisfying \(\Delta u = 0\)) can be added to a particular solution to yield another solution. A particular solution is \(u_p(r) = \frac{r^2}{4}\), which is radial. Adding a non-radial harmonic function like \(h(x, y) = x\) results in a non-radial solution \(u = \frac{r^2}{4} + x\), which still satisfies \(\Delta u = 1\). 4. **Conclusion**: Since non-radial harmonic functions can be added to radial solutions, and harmonic functions are not necessarily radial, the solution \(u(x)\) does not have to be radial. The lack of boundary conditions allows for non-unique solutions, some of which are non-radial. Thus, the answer is \(\boxed{No}\).

The lack of boundary conditions allows for non-unique solutions, some of which are non-radial. Thus, the answer is \(\boxed{No}\).

Any harmonic function (satisfying \(\Delta u = 0\)) can be added to a particular solution to yield another solution. A particular solution is \(u_p(r) = \frac{r^2}{4}\), which is radial. Adding a non-radial harmonic function like \(h(x, y) = x\) results in a non-radial solution \(u = \frac{r^2}{4} + x\), which still satisfies \(\Delta u = 1\). 4. **Conclusion**: Since non-radial harmonic functions can be added to radial solutions, and harmonic functions are not necessarily radial, the solution \(u(x)\) does not have to be radial. The lack of boundary conditions allows for non-unique solutions, some of which are non-radial. Thus, the answer is \(\boxed{No}\).

Harmonic functions in the unit ball do not have to be radial (e.g., \(x_1, x_2, \ldots\) in \(\mathbb{R}^n\)). 3. **Example with Non-Radial Solution**: Consider \(f(|x|) = 1\) in \(\mathbb{R}^2\). A particular solution is \(u_p(r) = \frac{r^2}{4}\), which is radial. Adding a non-radial harmonic function like \(h(x, y) = x\) results in a non-radial solution \(u = \frac{r^2}{4} + x\), which still satisfies \(\Delta u = 1\). 4. **Conclusion**: Since non-radial harmonic functions can be added to radial solutions, and harmonic functions are not necessarily radial, the solution \(u(x)\) does not have to be radial. The lack of boundary conditions allows for non-unique solutions, some of which are non-radial. Thus, the answer is \(\boxed{No}\).

do_single_rollout

Problem: Alice and Bob both go to a party which starts at 5:00. Each of them arrives at a random time between 5:00 and 6:00. What is the probability that the number of minutes Alice is late for the party plus the number of minutes Bob is late for the party is less than 45? Express your answer as a common fraction. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: We let the $x$-axis represent the time Alice arrives at the party, and we let the $y$-axis represent the time Bob arrives at the party. Then we shade in the area where the number of minutes Alice is late for the party plus the number of minutes Bob is late for the party is less than 45. [asy] draw((0,0)--(0,60)); draw((0,60)--(60,60)--(60,0)); draw((0,0)--(60,0)); label("5:00", (0,0), SW); label("6:00", (0,60), W); label("6:00", (60,0), S); fill((0,0)--(45,0)--(0,45)--cycle, gray(.7)); [/asy] If we let 1 unit be one minute, the area of the shaded region is $\frac{45^2}{2}$ square units and the whole area is 3600 square units. Therefore, the probability that a randomly chosen point will land in the shaded region is $\frac{45^2}{2\cdot 3600}=\boxed{\frac{9}{32}}$.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Problem: Alice and Bob both go to a party which starts at 5:00. Each of them arrives at a random time between 5:00 and 6:00. What is the probability that the number of minutes Alice is late for the party plus the number of minutes Bob is late for the party is less than 45? Express your answer as a common fraction. Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{1}

Reference Answer: We let the $x$-axis represent the time Alice arrives at the party, and we let the $y$-axis represent the time Bob arrives at the party. Then we shade in the area where the number of minutes Alice is late for the party plus the number of minutes Bob is late for the party is less than 45. [asy] draw((0,0)--(0,60)); draw((0,60)--(60,60)--(60,0)); draw((0,0)--(60,0)); label("5:00", (0,0), SW); label("6:00", (0,60), W); label("6:00", (60,0), S); fill((0,0)--(45,0)--(0,45)--cycle, gray(.7)); [/asy] If we let 1 unit be one minute, the area of the shaded region is $\frac{45^2}{2}$ square units and the whole area is 3600 square units. Therefore, the probability that a randomly chosen point will land in the shaded region is $\frac{45^2}{2\cdot 3600}=\boxed{\frac{9}{32}}$.
